<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;params=52_23_53_N_00_15_44_E_region:GB_type:city(20112)"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">52°23′53″N</span> <span class="longitude">00°15′44″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">&#xfeff; / &#xfeff;</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">52.39806°N 0.26222°E</span><span style="display:none">&#xfeff; / <span class="geo">52.39806; 0.26222</span></span></span></a></span><span style="font-size: small;"><span id="coordinates"><a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">Coordinates</a>: <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;params=52_23_53_N_00_15_44_E_region:GB_type:city(20112)"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">52°23′53″N</span> <span class="longitude">00°15′44″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">&#xfeff; / &#xfeff;</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">52.39806°N 0.26222°E</span><span style="display:none">&#xfeff; / <span class="geo">52.39806; 0.26222</span></span></span></a></span></span></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Ely</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span></span>/</a></span>&#32;<span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-uk-Ely.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a0/En-uk-Ely.ogg" class="internal" title="En-uk-Ely.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">EE</span>-lee</i></a>) is a <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_city" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral city">cathedral city</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cambridgeshire" title="Cambridgeshire">Cambridgeshire</a>, England, about 14 miles (23&#160;km) north-northeast of <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a> and about 80 miles (129&#160;km) by road from London. <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelthryth" title="Æthelthryth">Æthelthryth</a> (also known as Etheldreda) founded an abbey at Ely in 673; the abbey was destroyed in 870 by Danish invaders and was rebuilt by <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelwold_of_Winchester" title="Æthelwold of Winchester">Æthelwold</a>, Bishop of <a href="/wiki/Winchester" title="Winchester">Winchester</a>, in 970. Construction of the cathedral was started in 1083 by a <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Normans" title="Anglo-Normans">Norman</a> abbot, <a href="/wiki/Simeon_(abbot)" title="Simeon (abbot)">Simeon</a>. <a href="/wiki/Alan_of_Walsingham" title="Alan of Walsingham">Alan of Walsingham</a>'s octagon, built over Ely's <a href="/wiki/Nave" title="Nave">nave</a> <a href="/wiki/Crossing_(architecture)" title="Crossing (architecture)">crossing</a> between 1322 and 1328, is the "greatest individual achievement of architectural genius at Ely Cathedral", according to architectural historian <a href="/wiki/Nikolaus_Pevsner" title="Nikolaus Pevsner">Nikolaus Pevsner</a>. Building continued until the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">dissolution</a> of the abbey in 1539 during the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation#Church_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a>. The cathedral was sympathetically restored between 1845 and 1870 by the architect <a href="/wiki/George_Gilbert_Scott" title="George Gilbert Scott">George Gilbert Scott</a>. As the seat of a <a href="/wiki/Diocese" title="Diocese">diocese</a>, Ely has long been considered a city; in 1974, <a href="/wiki/City_status_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="City status in the United Kingdom">city status</a> was granted by <a href="/wiki/Royal_charter" title="Royal charter">royal charter</a>.
</p><p>Ely is built on a 23-square-mile (60&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) <a href="/wiki/Kimmeridge_Clay" title="Kimmeridge Clay">Kimmeridge Clay</a> island which, at 85 feet (26&#160;m), is the highest land in <a href="/wiki/The_Fens" title="The Fens">the Fens</a>. Major rivers including the <a href="/wiki/River_Witham" title="River Witham">Witham</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Welland" title="River Welland">Welland</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Nene" title="River Nene">Nene</a> and <a href="/wiki/River_Great_Ouse" title="River Great Ouse">Great Ouse</a> feed into the Fens and, until draining commenced in the 17th century, formed freshwater marshes and <a href="/wiki/Mere_(lake)" title="Mere (lake)">meres</a> within which peat was laid down. There are two <a href="/wiki/Site_of_Special_Scientific_Interest" title="Site of Special Scientific Interest">Sites of Special Scientific Interest</a> in the city: a former Kimmeridge Clay quarry, and one of the United Kingdom's best remaining examples of medieval <a href="/wiki/Ridge_and_furrow" title="Ridge and furrow">ridge and furrow</a> agriculture.
</p><p>The economy of the region is mainly agricultural. Before the Fens were drained, the harvesting of <a href="/wiki/Salix_viminalis" title="Salix viminalis">osier</a> (willow) and <a href="/wiki/Cyperaceae" title="Cyperaceae">sedge</a> (rush) and the extraction of peat were important activities, as were <a href="/wiki/Eel" title="Eel">eel</a> fishing—from which the settlement's name may have been derived—and <a href="/wiki/Fowling" title="Fowling">wildfowling</a>. The city had been the centre of local pottery production for more than 700 years, including pottery known as Babylon ware. A <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman road</a>, <a href="/wiki/Akeman_Street" title="Akeman Street">Akeman Street</a>, passes through the city; the southern end is at <a href="/wiki/Ermine_Street" title="Ermine Street">Ermine Street</a> near <a href="/wiki/Wimpole" title="Wimpole">Wimpole</a> and its northern end is at <a href="/wiki/Brancaster" title="Brancaster">Brancaster</a>. Little direct evidence of Roman occupation in Ely exists, although there are nearby Roman settlements such as those at <a href="/wiki/Little_Thetford" title="Little Thetford">Little Thetford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stretham" title="Stretham">Stretham</a>. A coach route, known to have existed in 1753 between Ely and Cambridge, was improved in 1769 as a <a href="/wiki/Turnpike_trust" class="mw-redirect" title="Turnpike trust">turnpike</a> (toll road). The present-day <a href="/wiki/A10_road_(England)" title="A10 road (England)">A10</a> closely follows this route; a southwestern bypass of the city was built in 1986. <a href="/wiki/Ely_railway_station" title="Ely railway station">Ely railway station</a>, built in 1845, is on the <a href="/wiki/Fen_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Fen Line">Fen Line</a> and is now a railway hub, with lines north to <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Lynn" title="King&#39;s Lynn">King's Lynn</a>, northwest to <a href="/wiki/Peterborough" title="Peterborough">Peterborough</a>, east to <a href="/wiki/Norwich" title="Norwich">Norwich</a>, southeast to <a href="/wiki/Ipswich" title="Ipswich">Ipswich</a> and south to Cambridge and London.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/The_King%27s_School,_Ely" class="mw-redirect" title="The King&#39;s School, Ely">King's School</a> is a coeducational boarding school which was granted a royal charter in 1541 by <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a>; the school claims to have existed since 970. <a href="/wiki/Henry_I_of_England" title="Henry I of England">Henry I</a> granted the first annual Fair, Saint&#160;Audrey's (or <a href="/wiki/Etheldreda" class="mw-redirect" title="Etheldreda">Etheldreda</a>'s) seven-day event, to the abbot and convent on 10 October 1189; the word "tawdry" originates from cheap lace sold at this fair. Present-day annual events include the Eel Festival in May, established in 2004, and a fireworks display in Ely Park, first staged in 1974. The city of Ely has been <a href="/wiki/Twin_towns_and_sister_cities" class="mw-redirect" title="Twin towns and sister cities">twinned</a> with Denmark's oldest town, <a href="/wiki/Ribe" title="Ribe">Ribe</a>, since 1956. <a href="/wiki/Ely_City_F.C." title="Ely City F.C.">Ely City Football Club</a> was formed in 1885.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-history">Pre-history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Roswell_Pits" title="Roswell Pits">Roswell Pits</a><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;i&#93;</a></sup> are a <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">palaeontologically</a> significant <a href="/wiki/Site_of_Special_Scientific_Interest" title="Site of Special Scientific Interest">Site of Special Scientific Interest</a> (SSSI) one mile (1.6&#160;km) northeast of the city. The Jurassic Kimmeridge Clays were quarried in the 19th and 20th centuries for the production of pottery and for maintenance of river embankments. Many specimens of <a href="/wiki/Ammonite" class="mw-redirect" title="Ammonite">ammonites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belemnite" class="mw-redirect" title="Belemnite">belemnites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bivalve" class="mw-redirect" title="Bivalve">bivalves</a> were found during quarrying, in addition to an almost complete specimen of a <a href="/wiki/Pliosaur" class="mw-redirect" title="Pliosaur">pliosaur</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is some scattered evidence of Late <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a><sup id="cite_ref-CHERCB15534_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHERCB15534-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_Britain" title="Bronze Age Britain">Bronze Age</a><sup id="cite_ref-CHERCB15553_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHERCB15553-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> activity in Ely such as <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> flint tools,<sup id="cite_ref-CHER10475_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHER10475-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> a Bronze Age axe<sup id="cite_ref-CHER08043_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHER08043-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and spearhead.<sup id="cite_ref-CHER09452_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHER09452-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> There is slightly denser <a href="/wiki/British_Iron_Age" title="British Iron Age">Iron Age</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman</a> activity with some evidence of at least seasonal occupation. For example, a possible farmstead, of the late Iron Age to early Roman period, was discovered at West Fen Road<sup id="cite_ref-CHERCB15472_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHERCB15472-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and some Roman pottery was found close to the east end of the cathedral on The Paddock.<sup id="cite_ref-CHER10170B_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHER10170B-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> There was a Roman settlement, including a tile kiln built over an earlier Iron Age settlement, in <a href="/wiki/Little_Thetford" title="Little Thetford">Little Thetford</a>, three miles (5&#160;km) to the south.<sup id="cite_ref-CHERCB15675_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHERCB15675-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The origin and meaning of Ely's name have always been regarded as obscure by place-name scholars, and are still disputed. The earliest record of the name is in the Latin text of Bede's <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_ecclesiastica_gentis_Anglorum" class="mw-redirect" title="Historia ecclesiastica gentis Anglorum">Historia ecclesiastica gentis Anglorum</a></i>, where <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a> wrote <i>Elge</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiles1843chap.&amp;nbsp;XIX_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiles1843chap.&amp;nbsp;XIX-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> This is apparently not a Latin name, and subsequent Latin texts nearly all used the forms <i>Elia</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrabb1833&lt;span_class=&quot;smallcaps&quot;&gt;&lt;span_style=&quot;font-variant:_small-caps;_text-transform:_lowercase;&quot;&gt;ELY&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrabb1833&lt;span_class=&quot;smallcaps&quot;&gt;&lt;span_style=&quot;font-variant:_small-caps;_text-transform:_lowercase;&quot;&gt;ELY&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <i>Eli</i>, or <i>Heli</i> with inorganic <i>H-</i>. In <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> charters, and in the <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i>, the spelling is usually <i>Elig</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarle1865161,_210,_242_et_al_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarle1865161,_210,_242_et_al-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Skeat derived the name Ely from what he called "O[ld] Northumbrian" <i>ēlġē</i>, meaning "district of <a href="/wiki/Eel" title="Eel">eels</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkeat190151–2_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkeat190151–2-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> This uses a hypothetical word <i>*ġē</i>, which is not recorded in isolation but thought by some to be related to the modern German word <i><a href="/wiki/Gau_(country_subdivision)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gau (country subdivision)">Gau</a></i>, meaning "district". The theory is that the name then developed a vowel to become <i>ēliġē</i>, and was afterwards re-interpreted to mean "eel island". This essentially is the explanation accepted by Reaney<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReaney1943213–4_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReaney1943213–4-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Ekwall,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEkwall1960166_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEkwall1960166-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Mills<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMills2003178_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills2003178-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and Watts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatts2004215_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatts2004215-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>But difficulties remain. Bailey, in his discussion of <i>ġē</i> names, has pointed out that Ely would be anomalous if really from <i>ēlġē</i> "eel district", being remote from the areas where possible examples of <i>ġē</i> names occur, and moreover, there is no parallel for the use of a fish-name in compounds with <i>ġē</i>. More seriously, the usual English spelling remains <i>Elig</i>, even in the <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative case</a> used after many prepositions, where <i>Elige</i> would be expected if the second element were <i>īġ</i> "island". This is in conflict with all the other island names which surround Ely.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBailey199963–76_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBailey199963–76-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_period">Medieval period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Medieval period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The city's origins lay in the foundation of an abbey in 673,<sup id="cite_ref-DoEHistory_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DoEHistory-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> one mile (1.6&#160;km) to the north of the village of <a href="/wiki/Cratendune" title="Cratendune">Cratendune</a> on the Isle of Ely, under the protection of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Etheldreda" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Etheldreda">Saint Etheldreda</a>, daughter of <a href="/wiki/Anna_of_East_Anglia" title="Anna of East Anglia">King Anna</a>. This first abbey was destroyed in 870 by Danish invaders<sup id="cite_ref-Geoglogy1_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geoglogy1-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and rededicated to Etheldreda in 970 by <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelwold_of_Winchester" title="Æthelwold of Winchester">Ethelwold</a>, Bishop of <a href="/wiki/Winchester" title="Winchester">Winchester</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The abbots of Ely then accumulated such wealth in the region that in the Domesday survey (1086) it was the "second richest monastery in England".<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Normans" title="Anglo-Normans">Norman</a> bishop, <a href="/wiki/Simeon_(abbot)" title="Simeon (abbot)">Simeon</a>, started building the cathedral in 1083.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The octagon was rebuilt by sacrist Alan of Walsingham between 1322 and 1328 after the collapse of the original nave crossing on 22&#160;February 1322.<sup id="cite_ref-Pevsner_1977_340_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pevsner_1977_340-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Ely's octagon is considered "one of the wonders of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval</a> world".<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Architectural historian <a href="/wiki/Nikolaus_Pevsner" title="Nikolaus Pevsner">Nikolaus Pevsner</a> believes the octagon "is a delight from beginning to end for anyone who feels for space as strongly as for construction" and is the "greatest individual achievement of architectural genius at Ely Cathedral".<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ely_Oliver_Cromwell_House.jpg" class="image"><img alt="East aspect of the Oliver Cromwell House in Ely" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Ely_Oliver_Cromwell_House.jpg/220px-Ely_Oliver_Cromwell_House.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Ely_Oliver_Cromwell_House.jpg/330px-Ely_Oliver_Cromwell_House.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Ely_Oliver_Cromwell_House.jpg/440px-Ely_Oliver_Cromwell_House.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ely_Oliver_Cromwell_House.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>East aspect of St&#160;Mary's vicarage, a Grade&#160;II* <a href="/wiki/Listed_building" title="Listed building">listed building</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CromwellHouse_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CromwellHouse-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> lived here between 1638 and 1646.<sup id="cite_ref-Dorman_1986_63_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dorman_1986_63-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Since 1990, the building has been used as <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell%27s_House" title="Oliver Cromwell&#39;s House">Oliver Cromwell's House</a> Tourist Attraction and tourist information centre.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cromwell1990_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cromwell1990-35">&#91;ii&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Cherry Hill is the site of <a href="/wiki/Ely_Castle" title="Ely Castle">Ely Castle</a> which is of Norman construction and is a United Kingdom <a href="/wiki/Scheduled_monument" title="Scheduled monument">scheduled monument</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CHER01764_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHER01764-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Of similar construction to <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Castle" title="Cambridge Castle">Cambridge Castle</a>, the 250-foot (76&#160;m) diameter, 40 feet (12&#160;m) high citadel-type <a href="/wiki/Motte_and_bailey" class="mw-redirect" title="Motte and bailey">motte and bailey</a> is thought to be a royal defence built by <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a> following submission of the Isle from rebels such as the <a href="/wiki/Morcar" title="Morcar">Earl Morcar</a> and the folk-hero <a href="/wiki/Hereward_the_Wake" title="Hereward the Wake">Hereward the Wake</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pevsner_1977_380_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pevsner_1977_380-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> This would date the first building of the castle to <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>&#160;1070.<sup id="cite_ref-CHER01764_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHER01764-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestant_martyrs">Protestant martyrs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Protestant martyrs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the accession of <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary I of England</a> to the throne in 1553, the papacy made its first effective efforts to enforce the <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_III" title="Pope Paul III">Pope Paul III</a>-initiated <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Catholic reforms</a> in England.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> During this time, which became known as the <a href="/wiki/Marian_Persecutions" class="mw-redirect" title="Marian Persecutions">Marian Persecutions</a>, two men from <a href="/wiki/Wisbech" title="Wisbech">Wisbech</a>, constable William Wolsey and painter Robert Pygot, "were accused of not&#160;… believing that the body and blood of Christ were present in the bread and wine of the sacrament of mass".<sup id="cite_ref-Blakeman131_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blakeman131-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> For this <a href="/wiki/Christian_heresy" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian heresy">Christian heresy</a> they were condemned by the bishop's chancellor, <a href="/wiki/John_Fuller_(college_head)" title="John Fuller (college head)">John Fuller</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> on the 9 October 1555.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> On 16 October 1555 they were <a href="/wiki/Death_by_burning" title="Death by burning">burnt at the stake</a> "probably on the Palace Green in front of Ely Cathedral".<sup id="cite_ref-Blakeman131_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blakeman131-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In <i>The Book of Ely</i> published in 1990, Blakeman writes that "permission was not given" for a memorial to the martyrs to be placed on Palace Green.<sup id="cite_ref-Blakeman131_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blakeman131-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In 2011, a plaque recording this martyrdom event was erected on the northeast corner of Palace Green by the City of Ely Perspective. The plaque is located 2 inches from the pavement floor in an obscure easily missed corner.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><br />
</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-medieval_decline">Post-medieval decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Post-medieval decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Speeds_plan_of_Ely_1610_from_Dorman_1986.jpg" class="image"><img alt="John Speed&#39;s plan of Ely, 1610" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Speeds_plan_of_Ely_1610_from_Dorman_1986.jpg/220px-Speeds_plan_of_Ely_1610_from_Dorman_1986.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Speeds_plan_of_Ely_1610_from_Dorman_1986.jpg/330px-Speeds_plan_of_Ely_1610_from_Dorman_1986.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Speeds_plan_of_Ely_1610_from_Dorman_1986.jpg/440px-Speeds_plan_of_Ely_1610_from_Dorman_1986.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2598" data-file-height="2371" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Speeds_plan_of_Ely_1610_from_Dorman_1986.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Earliest known map of Ely<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/John_Speed" title="John Speed">John Speed</a>, 1610.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;iii&#93;</a></sup> The cathedral is dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">St Peter</a> at this time and a windmill<sup id="cite_ref-CHER01764_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHER01764-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> is shown on Mount Hill where the post-conquest <a href="/wiki/Motte_and_bailey" class="mw-redirect" title="Motte and bailey">motte and bailey</a> <a href="/wiki/Ely_Castle" title="Ely Castle">Ely Castle</a> once stood. In the 18th century the Reverend <a href="/wiki/James_Bentham" title="James Bentham">James Bentham</a> planted trees on Mount Hill which was named Cherry Hill at least since 1821.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> lived in Ely from 1636 to 1646 after inheriting a sixteenth-century property—now known as <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell%27s_House" title="Oliver Cromwell&#39;s House">Oliver Cromwell's House</a>—and the position of local tax collector from his mother's brother, Sir Thomas Steward.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Cromwell was one of the governors of Thomas Parsons' Charity,<sup id="cite_ref-Dorman_1986_63_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dorman_1986_63-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> which dates back to 1445<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> and was granted a Royal Charter by <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I of England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The Charity still provides grants and housing to deserving local applicants.<sup id="cite_ref-Parsons_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parsons-51">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There was a form of early <a href="/wiki/Workhouse" title="Workhouse">workhouse</a> in 1687, perhaps at St&#160;Mary's, which may have been part of an arrangement made between the Ely people and a Nicholas Wythers of Norwich in 1675.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> He was paid £30 per annum to employ the poor to "spin jersey" and was to pay them in money not goods.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> A purpose-built workhouse was erected in 1725 for 35 inmates on what is now St&#160;Mary's Court. Four other workhouses existed, including Holy Trinity on Fore Hill for 80 inmates (1738–1956) and the Ely Union workhouse, built in 1837, which housed up to 300 inmates. The latter became Tower Hospital in 1948 and is now a residential building, Tower Court. Two other former workhouses were the Haven Quayside for unmarried mothers and another on the site of what is now the Hereward Hall in Silver Street.<sup id="cite_ref-cambridgeshire43_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cambridgeshire43-54">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The diaries of writers and journalists such as <a href="/wiki/William_Camden" title="William Camden">William Camden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celia_Fiennes" title="Celia Fiennes">Celia Fiennes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Defoe" title="Daniel Defoe">Daniel Defoe</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Byng,_5th_Viscount_Torrington" title="John Byng, 5th Viscount Torrington">John Byng</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Cobbett" title="William Cobbett">William Cobbett</a> illustrate the decline of Ely after the 14th century <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">plague</a> and the 16th century <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">reformation</a> which led to the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">dissolution</a> of the monastery in 1539.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In the 1607 edition of <i>Britannia</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;iv&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chorography" title="Chorography">chorographic</a> surveyor William Camden records that "as for Ely it selfe, it is no small Citie, or greatly to be counted off either for beauty or frequency and resort, as having an unwholsome aire by reason of the fens round about".<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;v&#93;</a></sup> In 1698, Celia Fiennes was writing "the Bishop [<a href="/wiki/Simon_Patrick" title="Simon Patrick">Simon Patrick</a>] does not Care to stay long in this place not being for his health&#160;… they have lost their Charter&#160;… and its a shame [the Bishop] does not see it better ordered and y<sup>e</sup> buildings and streetes put in a better Condition. They are a slothful people and for little but y<sup>e</sup> takeing Care of their Grounds and Cattle w<sup>ch</sup> is of vast advantage".<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Daniel Defoe, when writing in the Eastern Counties section of <i><a href="/wiki/A_tour_thro%27_the_whole_island_of_Great_Britain" title="A tour thro&#39; the whole island of Great Britain">A tour thro' the whole island of Great Britain</a></i> (1722), went  "to Ely, whose cathedral, standing in a level flat country, is seen far and wide&#160;… that some of it is so antient, totters so much with every gust of wind, looks so like a decay, and seems so near it, that when ever it does fall, all that 'tis likely will be thought strange in it, will be, that it did not fall a hundred years sooner".<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Howard_(prison_reformer)" title="John Howard (prison reformer)">John Howard (prison reformer)</a> visited Ely and described the conditions in The Gaol:- 
'This gaol the property ofthe bishop, who is lord of the franchise
of the Isle of Ely, was in part rebuilt by the late bishop about ten years ago; upon complaint of the cruel method* which for want of a safe gaol, the Keeper took to secure his prisoners (*This was by chaining them down upon their backs on a floor, across which were several iron bars and iron collar with spikes about their neck). The gaoler John Allday did not receive a salary'. He records that the number of debtors outnumbered the number of felons in the prison.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On his way to a Midlands tour, John Byng visited Ely on 5&#160;July 1790 staying at the Lamb Inn.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In his diary<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;vi&#93;</a></sup> he writes that "the town [Ely] is mean, to the extreme&#160;… those withdrawn, their dependancies must decay".<sup id="cite_ref-DormanAkerman_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DormanAkerman-63">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Recording in his <i><a href="/wiki/Rural_Rides" title="Rural Rides">Rural Rides</a></i> on 25&#160;March 1830, William Cobbett reports that "Ely is what one may call a miserable little town: very prettily situated, but poor and mean. Everything seems to be on the decline, as, indeed, is the case everywhere, where the clergy are the masters".<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ely_and_Littleport_riots_1816" class="mw-redirect" title="Ely and Littleport riots 1816">Ely and Littleport riots</a> occurred between 22 and 24&#160;May 1816. At the Special Commission assizes, held at Ely between 17 and 22&#160;June 1816, twenty-four rioters were condemned. Nineteen had their sentences variously commuted from <a href="/wiki/Penal_transportation" title="Penal transportation">penal transportation</a> for life to twelve-months imprisonment; the remaining five were executed on 28&#160;June 1816.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Victorian_and_twentieth-century_regeneration">Victorian and twentieth-century regeneration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Victorian and twentieth-century regeneration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ely_the_market_place-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Watercolour The Market Place, Ely by W. W. Collins published 1908 showing northeast aspect of Ely Cathedral in the background with the Almonry in front of that and the now demolished corn exchange building to the right of the picture" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/26/Ely_the_market_place-1.jpg/220px-Ely_the_market_place-1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/26/Ely_the_market_place-1.jpg/330px-Ely_the_market_place-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/26/Ely_the_market_place-1.jpg/440px-Ely_the_market_place-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1518" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ely_the_market_place-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Market Place, Ely</i>, pencil and <a href="/wiki/Watercolor_painting" title="Watercolor painting">watercolour</a> by <a href="/wiki/William_Wiehe_Collins_(painter)" class="mw-redirect" title="William Wiehe Collins (painter)">W. W. Collins</a><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> published 1908<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> showing northeast aspect of <a href="/wiki/Ely_Cathedral" title="Ely Cathedral">Ely Cathedral</a> in the background with the <a href="/wiki/Almonry" title="Almonry">Almonry</a><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>—now a restaurant and art gallery<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>—in front of that and the 1847 <a href="/wiki/Corn_exchange" title="Corn exchange">corn exchange</a> building,<sup id="cite_ref-Pevsner_1977_380_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pevsner_1977_380-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> now demolished, to the right of the picture.</div></div></div>
<p>Ely Cathedral was "the first great cathedral to be thoroughly restored".<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Work commenced in 1845 and was completed nearly thirty years later; most of the work was "sympathetically" carried out by the architect <a href="/wiki/George_Gilbert_Scott" title="George Gilbert Scott">George Gilbert Scott</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The only pavement <a href="/wiki/Labyrinth" title="Labyrinth">labyrinth</a> to be found in an English cathedral was installed below the west tower in 1870.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For over 800 years the cathedral and its associated buildings—built on an elevation 68 feet (21&#160;m) above the nearby fens—have visually influenced the city and its surrounding area. Geographer John Jones, writing in 1924, reports that "from the roof of King's Chapel in Cambridge, on a clear day, Ely [cathedral] can be seen on the horizon, 16 miles (26&#160;km) distant, an expression of the flatness of the fens".<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In 1954, architectural historian Nikolaus Pevsner wrote "as one approaches Ely on foot or on a bicycle, or perhaps in an open car, the cathedral dominates the picture for miles around&#160;… and offers from everywhere an outline different from that of any other English cathedral".<sup id="cite_ref-Pevsnerview_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pevsnerview-76">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Local historian Pamela Blakeman reports a claim that "Grouped around [the cathedral]&#160;… is the largest collection of mediaeval buildings still in daily use in this country".<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III of England</a> granted a <a href="/wiki/Market_town" title="Market town">market</a> to the Bishop of Ely using <a href="/wiki/Letters_close" title="Letters close">letters close</a> on 9&#160;April 1224<sup id="cite_ref-Fairs_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fairs-78">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> although Ely had been a trading centre prior to this.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Present weekly market days are Thursday and Saturday and seasonal markets are held monthly on Sundays and Bank Holiday Mondays from Easter to November. The city is situated on the <a href="/wiki/River_Great_Ouse" title="River Great Ouse">River Great Ouse</a>, which was a significant means of transport until the fens were drained and Ely ceased to be an island in the eighteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The river is now a popular boating spot, and has a large marina.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberty_of_Ely">Liberty of Ely</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Liberty of Ely">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The abbey at Ely was one of many which were refounded in the Benedictine reforms of King <a href="/wiki/Edgar_the_Peaceful" title="Edgar the Peaceful">Edgar the Peaceful</a> (943–975).<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The "special and peculiarly ancient"<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> honour and freedoms given to Ely by charter at that time<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> may have been intended to award only fiscal privilege,<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> but have been interpreted to confer on subsequent bishops the authority and power of a ruler.<sup id="cite_ref-Liberty_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liberty-85">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> These rights were reconfirmed in charters granted by <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a> and in <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a>'s confirmation of the old English <a href="/wiki/Liberty_(division)" title="Liberty (division)">liberty</a> at Kenford.<sup id="cite_ref-Liberty_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liberty-85">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The Isle of Ely was mentioned in some statutes<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;vii&#93;</a></sup> as a <a href="/wiki/County_palatine" title="County palatine">county palatine</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;viii&#93;</a></sup> this provided an explanation of the bishop's royal privileges and judicial authority, which would normally belong to the sovereign; but legal authorities such as <a href="/wiki/Sir_Edward_Coke" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Edward Coke">Sir Edward Coke</a> did not completely endorse the form of words.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> These bishop's rights were not fully extinguished until 1837.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_government">Local government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Local government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The local authority of the city was the <a href="/wiki/City_of_Ely_Urban_District_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="City of Ely Urban District Council">City of Ely Urban District Council</a> from 1894 to 1974. The <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Ely_County_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Isle of Ely County Council">Isle of Ely County Council</a> governed the Isle of Ely administrative county that surrounding and included the city from 1889 to 1965. In 1965 there was a reform of local government that merged the county council with that of Cambridgeshire to form the <a href="/wiki/Cambridgeshire_and_Isle_of_Ely_County_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Cambridgeshire and Isle of Ely County Council">Cambridgeshire and Isle of Ely County Council</a>. In 1974 as part of a national reform of local government, the Cambridgeshire and Isle of Ely County Council merged with the <a href="/wiki/Huntingdon_and_Peterborough_County_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Huntingdon and Peterborough County Council">Huntingdon and Peterborough County Council</a> to form the <a href="/wiki/Cambridgeshire_County_Council" title="Cambridgeshire County Council">Cambridgeshire County Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LocalGov1972_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LocalGov1972-91">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The City of Ely Urban District Council became the <a href="/wiki/City_of_Ely_Council" title="City of Ely Council">City of Ely Council</a>, a parish council which covers the same area but with fewer powers, and the <a href="/wiki/East_Cambridgeshire_District_Council" title="East Cambridgeshire District Council">East Cambridgeshire District Council</a> which covers a wider area.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="City_status">City status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: City status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/City_status_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="City status in the United Kingdom">City status in the United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_smallest_cities_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of smallest cities in the United Kingdom">List of smallest cities in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:287px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ely.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Ely.jpg/285px-Ely.jpg" decoding="async" width="285" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Ely.jpg/428px-Ely.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Ely.jpg/570px-Ely.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2920" data-file-height="1841" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ely.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>OpenMap of Ely demonstrating city boundary and environs. <div class="legend-line"><span style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:middle; width:1.67em; height:0px; border-style:none; border-top:2px dotted #000; border-top:2px solid orange;">&#160;</span>&#160;City/parish boundary</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #606060; background-color: #DFDFDF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Built-up area</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #606060; background-color: #F1EFEB; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Countryside</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #606060; background-color: #D0EFD0; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Greenfield land</div></div></div></div>
<p>As the <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_see" title="Episcopal see">seat</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Diocese" title="Diocese">diocese</a>, Ely has long been considered a city, holding the status by <a href="/wiki/Custom_(law)" class="mw-redirect" title="Custom (law)">ancient prescriptive right</a>: the caption to <a href="/wiki/John_Speed" title="John Speed">John Speed</a>'s 1610 plan of Ely<sup id="cite_ref-Speed1610_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speed1610-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> reads "Although this Citie of Ely", and Aikin refers to Ely as a city in 1800.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Ely, however, was not formally granted city status until 1&#160;April 1974 by Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth&#160;II</a> by <a href="/wiki/Letters_patent" title="Letters patent">letters patent</a>, to its civil parish.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Ely's population of 20,256 (as recorded in 2011)<sup id="cite_ref-Cambs2011_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cambs2011-94">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> classifies it as one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_the_United_Kingdom#England" title="List of cities in the United Kingdom">smallest cities in England</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> although the population has increased noticeably since 1991 when it was recorded at 11,291. Its urban area brings Ely into the top ten of smallest sized cities (1.84&#160;sq&#160;mi—4.77&#160;km<sup>2</sup>), but by city council area it is much larger in coverage (22.86&#160;sq&#160;mi—59.21&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) than many others.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Regular elections take place to the City of Ely Council, East Cambridgeshire District Council and Cambridgeshire County Council. The civil parish is divided into four wards called Ely North, Ely South, Ely East and Ely West. Fourteen councillors are elected to the parish council. The East Cambridgeshire District Council is also based in Ely.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> For elections to the East Cambridgeshire District Council the four wards of Ely South, Ely East and Ely West each return two district councillors; and Ely North returns three.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In 2011, the seats were held by four <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrat</a> and five <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> councillors.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> For elections to the Cambridgeshire County Council the city returns two councillors, both of whom were Conservatives in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> For elections to the UK Parliament, Ely is part of the <a href="/wiki/South_East_Cambridgeshire_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="South East Cambridgeshire (UK Parliament constituency)">South East Cambridgeshire</a> constituency.<sup id="cite_ref-HansardCambridgeshire_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HansardCambridgeshire-101">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> For elections to the European Parliament, Ely is part of the <a href="/wiki/East_of_England_(European_Parliament_constituency)" title="East of England (European Parliament constituency)">East of England</a> constituency.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/The_Fens" title="The Fens">The Fens</a></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology_and_topography">Geology and topography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Geology and topography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Isle_of_Ely_1648_by_J_Blaeu.jpg" class="image"><img alt="old yellowing map of east Cambridgeshire showing Isle of Ely surrounded by water" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Isle_of_Ely_1648_by_J_Blaeu.jpg/170px-Isle_of_Ely_1648_by_J_Blaeu.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Isle_of_Ely_1648_by_J_Blaeu.jpg/255px-Isle_of_Ely_1648_by_J_Blaeu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Isle_of_Ely_1648_by_J_Blaeu.jpg/340px-Isle_of_Ely_1648_by_J_Blaeu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4936" data-file-height="6336" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Isle_of_Ely_1648_by_J_Blaeu.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1648 drainage map showing the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Ely" title="Isle of Ely">Isle of Ely</a> still surrounded by water<br /><a href="/wiki/Joan_Blaeu" title="Joan Blaeu">Joan Blaeu</a> (1648) <i>Regiones Inundatae</i></div></div></div>
<p>The west of Cambridgeshire is made up of <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestones</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> period, whilst the east Cambridgeshire area consists of <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> (upper <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a>) <a href="/wiki/Chalk" title="Chalk">chalks</a> known locally as <a href="/wiki/Clunch" title="Clunch">clunch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In between these two major formations, the high ground forming the Isle of Ely is from a lower division Cretaceous system known as <a href="/wiki/Lower_Greensand" class="mw-redirect" title="Lower Greensand">Lower Greensand</a> which is capped by <a href="/wiki/Boulder_Clay" class="mw-redirect" title="Boulder Clay">Boulder Clay</a>; all local settlements, such as <a href="/wiki/Stretham" title="Stretham">Stretham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Littleport,_Cambridgeshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Littleport, Cambridgeshire">Littleport</a>, are on similar islands. These islands rise above the surrounding flat land which forms the largest plain of Britain<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;ix&#93;</a></sup> from the Jurassic system of partly consolidated clays or muds.<sup id="cite_ref-Geoglogy1_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geoglogy1-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Kimmeridge Clay beds dipping gently west underlie the Lower Greensand of the area exposed, for example, about one mile (2&#160;km) south of Ely in the <a href="/wiki/Roswell_Pits" title="Roswell Pits">Roswell Pits</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The Lower Greensand is partly capped by <a href="/wiki/Till" title="Till">glacial deposits</a> forming the highest point in <a href="/wiki/East_Cambridgeshire" title="East Cambridgeshire">East Cambridgeshire</a>, rising to 85 feet (26&#160;m) above sea level in Ely.<sup id="cite_ref-BGS173_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BGS173-106">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The low-lying fens surrounding the island of Ely were formed, prior to the 17th century, by alternate fresh-water and sea-water incursions. Major rivers in the region, including the <a href="/wiki/River_Witham" title="River Witham">Witham</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Welland" title="River Welland">Welland</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Nene" title="River Nene">Nene</a> and <a href="/wiki/River_Great_Ouse" title="River Great Ouse">Great Ouse</a>, drain an area of some 6,000 square miles (16,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>)—five times larger than the fens—into the basin that forms the fens.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Defoe in 1774 described the fens as "the sink of no less than thirteen Counties".<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> On 23&#160;November of that year, Church of England cleric and Christian theologician <a href="/wiki/John_Wesley" title="John Wesley">John Wesley</a>, wrote of his approach to Ely after visiting Norwich: "about eight, Wednesday, 23, Mr. Dancer met me with a chaise [carriage] and carried me to Ely. Oh, what want of common sense! Water covered the high road for a mile and a half. I asked, 'How must foot-people come to the town?' 'Why, they must wade through!<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>"<sup id="cite_ref-Wesley_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wesley-109">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">Peat</a> formed in the fresh-water swamps and meres whilst silts were deposited by the slow-moving sea-water.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Francis_Russell,_4th_Earl_of_Bedford" title="Francis Russell, 4th Earl of Bedford">Francis Russell, Earl of Bedford</a>, supported by Parliament, financed the draining of the fens during the 17th century, led by the Dutch engineer <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Vermuyden" title="Cornelius Vermuyden">Cornelius Vermuyden</a>; the fens continue to be drained to this day.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With an average annual rainfall of 24 inches (600&#160;mm), Cambridgeshire is one of the driest counties in the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>. Protected from the cool onshore coastal breezes east of the region, Cambridgeshire is warm in summer and cold and frosty in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Regional <a href="/wiki/Weather_forecasting" title="Weather forecasting">weather forecasting</a> and historical summaries are available from the UK <a href="/wiki/Met_Office" title="Met Office">Met Office</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The nearest Met Office <a href="/wiki/Weather_station" title="Weather station">weather station</a> is Cambridge.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;x&#93;</a></sup> Additional local weather stations report periodic figures to the internet such as <a href="/wiki/Weather_Underground_(weather_service)" title="Weather Underground (weather service)">Weather Underground</a>, Inc.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demography">Demography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Demography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday</a> survey of 1086 revealed 110 households<sup id="cite_ref-MedievalPopulation_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedievalPopulation-120">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> which were mainly rural.<sup id="cite_ref-VCH34_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VCH34-121">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> In 1251, a survey showed an increase to 345 households<sup id="cite_ref-MedievalPopulation_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedievalPopulation-120">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> with the start of urban living although still largely rural.<sup id="cite_ref-VCH34_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VCH34-121">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> By the 1416 survey there were 457 occupied premises in the city and many of the streets were arranged much as they are today.<sup id="cite_ref-VCH34_121-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VCH34-121">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> See also the cartographer John Speed's plan of Ely, 1610.<sup id="cite_ref-Speed1610_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speed1610-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In 1563 there were 800 households and by 1753 the population was recorded as 3,000.<sup id="cite_ref-MedievalPopulation_120-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedievalPopulation-120">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="text-align:center;">
