<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As an island surrounded by marshes and meres, the fishing of eels was important as both a food and an income for the abbot and his nearby tenants. For example, to the abbot of Ely in 1086, <i><a href="/wiki/Stuntney" title="Stuntney">Stuntenei</a></i> was worth 24,000 eels, <i><a href="/wiki/Littleport,_Cambridgeshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Littleport, Cambridgeshire">Litelport</a></i> 17,000 eels and even the small village of <i><a href="/wiki/Little_Thetford" title="Little Thetford">Liteltetford</a></i> was worth 3,250 eels.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Prior to the extensive and largely successful drainage of the fens during the seventeenth century, Ely was a trade centre for goods made out of willow, reeds and rushes and wild fowling was a major local activity.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Peat in the form of "turf" was used as a fuel and in the form of "moor" as a building material.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;xii&#93;</a></sup> Ampthill Clay was dug from the local area for the maintenance of river banks and Kimmeridge Clay at Roswell Pits for the making of pottery wares.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In general, from a geological perspective, "The district is almost entirely agricultural and has always been so. The only mineral worked at the present time is gravel for aggregate, although chalk, brick clay (Ampthill and Kimmeridge clays), phosphate (from Woburn Sands, Gault and Cambridge Greensand), sand and gravel, and peat have been worked on a small scale in the past".<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">Phosphate</a> nodules, referred to locally as <a href="/wiki/Coprolite" title="Coprolite">coprolites</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;xiii&#93;</a></sup> were dug in the area surrounding Ely between 1850 and 1890 for use as an <a href="/wiki/History_of_fertilizer" title="History of fertilizer">agricultural fertiliser</a>. This industry provided significant employment for the local labour force.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> One of the largest <a href="/wiki/Sugar_beet" title="Sugar beet">sugar beet</a> factories in England was opened in <a href="/wiki/Queen_Adelaide,_Cambridgeshire" title="Queen Adelaide, Cambridgeshire">Queen Adelaide</a>, two miles (3&#160;km) from the centre of Ely, in 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> The factory closed in 1981, although sugar beet is still farmed locally.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pottery was made in Ely from the 12th century until 1860:<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> records show around 80 people who classed their trade as potters.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> "Babylon ware" is the name given to pottery made in one area of Ely. This ware is thought to be so named because there were potters in an area cut off from the centre by the re-routing of the <a href="/wiki/River_Great_Ouse" title="River Great Ouse">River Great Ouse</a> around 1200; by the seventeenth century this area had become known as Babylon. Although the reason for the name is unclear, by 1850 it was in official use on maps. The building of the Ely to King's Lynn railway in 1847 cut the area off even further, and the inhabitants could only cross to Ely by boat.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eel_day_parade.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Eel day parade view down Fore Hill showing people in white Eel day tee-shirts walking towards the camera" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Eel_day_parade.jpg/170px-Eel_day_parade.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Eel_day_parade.jpg/255px-Eel_day_parade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Eel_day_parade.jpg/340px-Eel_day_parade.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eel_day_parade.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eel day carnival procession down Fore Hill, 2007</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Annual_events">Annual events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Annual events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Annual <a href="/wiki/Fair" title="Fair">fairs</a> have been held in Ely since the twelfth century.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Saint&#160;Audrey's (<a href="/wiki/Etheldreda" class="mw-redirect" title="Etheldreda">Etheldreda</a>'s) seven-day fair, held either side of 23&#160;June, was first granted officially by <a href="/wiki/Henry_I_of_England" title="Henry I of England">Henry I</a> to the abbot and convent on 10 October 1189.<sup id="cite_ref-Fairs_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fairs-78">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> At this fair, cheap necklaces, made from brightly coloured silk, were sold—these were called "tawdry lace".<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> "Tawdry", a corruption of "Saint Audrey", now means "pertaining to the nature of cheap and gaudy finery".<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Two other fairs, the 15‑day festival of St&#160;Lambert, first granted in 1312 and the 22‑day fair beginning on the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Ascension" title="Feast of the Ascension">Vigil of the Ascension</a>, first granted in 1318.<sup id="cite_ref-Fairs_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fairs-78">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The festival of St&#160;Lambert had stopped by the eighteenth&#160;century. St&#160;Etheldreda's and the Vigil of the Ascension markets still continue, although the number of days have been considerably reduced and the dates have changed.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Present-day annual events in Ely include Aquafest, which has been staged at the riverside by the Rotary Club on the first Sunday of July since 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;xiv&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aquafest_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aquafest-144">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Other events include the <a href="/wiki/Ely_Eel_Day" title="Ely Eel Day">Eel Day</a> carnival procession<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> and the annual fireworks display in Ely Park, first staged in 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-EPFireworks_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPFireworks-146">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> The Ely Folk Festival has been held in the city since 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-EPFolk_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPFolk-147">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The Ely Horticultural Society have been staging their Great Autumn Show since 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> In 2018 Ely hosted the 'Pride' festival, celebrating LGBT and diversity. At the inaugural festival 'For The Hornets' headlined and the cathedral flew the pride rainbow flag.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Twin_town">Twin town</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Twin town">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since September 1956, Ely has been <a href="/wiki/Twin_towns_and_sister_cities" class="mw-redirect" title="Twin towns and sister cities">twinned</a> with <a href="/wiki/Ribe" title="Ribe">Ribe</a>, Denmark's oldest town<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> and part of the <a href="/wiki/Esbjerg_Municipality" title="Esbjerg Municipality">Municipality of Esbjerg</a>; officials from Ribe first came to Ely in 1957. The golden anniversary of this twinning was celebrated in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Exchange visits occur roughly every two years.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Landmarks">Landmarks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Landmarks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Canonical_countershoted_castellated_cathedral_over_culverin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Cannon on the Palace green" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Canonical_countershoted_castellated_cathedral_over_culverin.jpg/170px-Canonical_countershoted_castellated_cathedral_over_culverin.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Canonical_countershoted_castellated_cathedral_over_culverin.jpg/255px-Canonical_countershoted_castellated_cathedral_over_culverin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Canonical_countershoted_castellated_cathedral_over_culverin.jpg/340px-Canonical_countershoted_castellated_cathedral_over_culverin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2241" data-file-height="3106" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Canonical_countershoted_castellated_cathedral_over_culverin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This cannon was captured during the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Sevastopol_(1854%E2%80%931855)" title="Siege of Sevastopol (1854–1855)">Siege of Sevastopol (1854–1855)</a><sup id="cite_ref-Cannon_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cannon-153">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> and was presented to the people of Ely by <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> in 1860. It is located on the Palace Green west of <a href="/wiki/Ely_Cathedral" title="Ely Cathedral">Ely Cathedral</a> and northwest of the Bishop's Palace.<sup id="cite_ref-Inscription_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Inscription-154">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_memorial">War memorial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: War memorial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A cannon, captured during the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Sevastopol_(1854%E2%80%931855)" title="Siege of Sevastopol (1854–1855)">Siege of Sevastopol (1854–1855)</a> and given to Ely by <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> in 1860, is located on Palace Green, west of the cathedral.<sup id="cite_ref-Cannon_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cannon-153">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> The inscription reads "Russian cannon captured during the Crimean War presented to the people of Ely by Queen Victoria in 1860 to mark the creation of the Ely Rifle Volunteers".<sup id="cite_ref-Inscription_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Inscription-154">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The cannon was cast<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;xv&#93;</a></sup> at the Alexandrovski factory in 1802, the factory's director being the Englishman, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Gascoigne" title="Charles Gascoigne">Charles Gascoigne</a>. The serial number is 8726. The calibre is 30 pounds (14&#160;kg) and the weight is 252 <a href="/wiki/Pood" title="Pood">poods</a>, or about 9,000 pounds (4,100&#160;kg).<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The cannon is mounted on an iron carriage which would previously have been mounted on a "heavy iron traversing slide" known as 'Systeme Venglov 1853'.<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter_1993_147_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter_1993_147-155">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The Ely <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_Force_(Great_Britain)" class="mw-redirect" title="Volunteer Force (Great Britain)">Rifle Volunteers</a>, formed in 1860, became part of the <a href="/wiki/Cambridgeshire_Regiment" title="Cambridgeshire Regiment">Cambridgeshire Regiment</a> during 1914–1918 then subsequently part of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Anglian_Regiment" title="Royal Anglian Regiment">Royal Anglian Regiment</a> until disbanded in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_buildings">Notable buildings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Notable buildings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Listed_buildings_in_Ely,_Cambridgeshire" title="Listed buildings in Ely, Cambridgeshire">Listed buildings in Ely</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ely_Cathedral" title="Ely Cathedral">Ely Cathedral</a></div>
<p>There are twenty three Grade&#160;I, six Grade&#160;II* and one hundred and fifty three Grade&#160;II <a href="/wiki/Listed_building" title="Listed building">listed buildings</a><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> in the city of Ely.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Cherry Hill, to the south of Cathedral Park, is the remains of the Norman period, motte and bailey, <a href="/wiki/Ely_Castle" title="Ely Castle">Ely Castle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ElyCity_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ElyCity-162">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The earliest written record of this 40-foot-high (12&#160;m) by 250-foot-diameter (76&#160;m) castle is in the time of <a href="/wiki/Henry_I_of_England" title="Henry I of England">Henry I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pevsner_1977_380_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pevsner_1977_380-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Two twelfth century hospitals, St&#160;Mary Magdalene founded 1172 and St&#160;John the Baptist founded <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>&#160;1200, were on the site of what is now a four-building farmstead. Building dates are not known but the extant remains indicate <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>&#160;1175–85.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hugh_of_Northwold" title="Hugh of Northwold">Bishop Northwold</a> merged the two hospitals in 1240. The farmstead Grade&#160;I listed building status was graded on 23&#160;September 1950 between four properties: St&#160;John's farmhouse,<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> a barn to the southwest (formerly chapel of St&#160;John),<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> a barn to the north (formerly chapel of St&#160;Mary)<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Dovecote" title="Dovecote">dovecote</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Above the north doorway of the southwestern barn of St&#160;John's farmhouse is a carved <a href="/wiki/Barnack_stone" class="mw-redirect" title="Barnack stone">Barnack stone</a> which is built into the thirteenth century wall. The stone is thought to have been robbed from the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon">Anglo-Saxon</a> monastery of St Etheldreda.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;xvi&#93;</a></sup> This heavily weathered eighth-century stone shows a man blowing a horn whilst riding on an ox.<sup id="cite_ref-PastscapeBarn_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PastscapeBarn-170">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Alcock_(bishop)" title="John Alcock (bishop)">John Alcock</a>, Bishop of Ely and founder of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_College,_Cambridge" title="Jesus College, Cambridge">Jesus College, Cambridge</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> constructed the Bishop's Palace during his bishopric, between 1486 and 1500;<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> of the original fabric, only the east tower and the lower part of the west tower remain.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> A "startlingly huge" London Plane tree, planted in 1680, still grows in the garden and is "said to be one of the largest in England".<sup id="cite_ref-Pevsner_1977_379_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pevsner_1977_379-175">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Lany" title="Benjamin Lany">Benjamin Lany</a>, Bishop of Ely from 1667 until 1675, demolished much of Alcock's work and thus became responsible for most of the present-day building.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> This Grade&#160;I listed building is southwest of and close to the west end of the cathedral, opposite the original village green, now named Palace Green.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell%27s_House" title="Oliver Cromwell&#39;s House">St Mary's Vicarage</a>, better known locally as Cromwell's House, is a Grade&#160;II* listed building of mainly sixteenth-century <a href="/wiki/Timber_framing" title="Timber framing">plaster-frame</a> construction although there exist some stone arches, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>&#160;1380. A plaque on the front of the house records that this is "Cromwell House, the residence of Oliver Cromwell from 1636 to 1647 when collector of Ely Tithes".<sup id="cite_ref-CromwellHouse_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CromwellHouse-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Between 1843 and 1847 the house was the <i>Cromwell Arms</i> public house and it was restored in 1905 when it was given its "timbered appearance".<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> The house was opened as a re-creation of seventeenth-century living and a tourist information centre on 6 December 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-Cromwell1990_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cromwell1990-35">&#91;ii&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The former Ely <a href="/wiki/Prison" title="Prison">Gaol</a> is a late seventeenth-century Grade&#160;II listed building<sup id="cite_ref-ElyMuseum_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ElyMuseum-181">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> which since has been the Ely museum.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> From the thirteenth century, buildings on this site have been; a private house, a tavern and—since 1836 when the Bishop transferred his thirteenth-century prison from Ely Porta—the Bishop's Gaol.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> It was a registry office prior to becoming a museum.<sup id="cite_ref-ElyMuseum_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ElyMuseum-181">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Maltings is another of Ely's distinguishing buildings. Built in 1868 as part of Ebenezer William Harlock's brewery complex, the Maltings was used to process locally grown barley into Malt for brewing.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> The Maltings is located on Ely's Waterside and has since left its brewing days behind. It is now a venue that hosts live events and entertainment as well as private functions such as weddings and business conferences.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
The Maltings is also home to the Ely-Ribe Tapestry.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> The Ely-Ribe Tapestry was commissioned in 2004 to mark the 50th Anniversary of the twinning of the two towns; Ely in Cambridgeshire England and Ribe in Jutland, Denmark.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The designer, Ullrich described the Tapestry as "a portrait of two different cities in two different countries".<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Lamb Hotel is a Grade&#160;II listed building which is prominently situated on the corner of Lynn Road and High Street 100 yards (91&#160;m) north of the west end of the cathedral.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> The hotel was erected as a <a href="/wiki/Coaching_inn" title="Coaching inn">coaching house</a> on the site of the previous Lamb Inn during 1828 and 1829. At that time it had stabling for 30&#160;horses and a lock-up for two coaches.<sup id="cite_ref-Ashton_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashton-190">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> In 1906 it had five bedrooms for the landlord, 15 rooms for lodgers, room for 15 horses and 12 vehicles. In 2007 it had 31 rooms for guests.<sup id="cite_ref-Ashton_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashton-190">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> It is claimed that an inn has existed on the site since Bishop Fordham's survey between 1416 and 1417.<sup id="cite_ref-Holmes_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes-191">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> It is also claimed that an inn existed on the site in 1690, but no earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The city's courthouse was built in 1821, replacing a previous court in the Shire Hall.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> It ceased operation in 2011 as part of central government measures to close 93 <a href="/wiki/Magistrates%27_Court_(England_and_Wales)" class="mw-redirect" title="Magistrates&#39; Court (England and Wales)">magistrates' courts</a> across England and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_sites">Notable sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Notable sites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ely_Castle" title="Ely Castle">Ely Castle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roswell_Pits" title="Roswell Pits">Roswell Pits</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chettisham_Meadow" title="Chettisham Meadow">Chettisham Meadow</a></div>
<p>The former Kimmeridge Clay quarry <a href="/wiki/Roswell_Pits" title="Roswell Pits">Roswell Pits</a>, one mile (1.6&#160;km) southwest of Ely Cathedral, is now a nature reserve and <a href="/wiki/Site_of_Special_Scientific_Interest" title="Site of Special Scientific Interest">Site of Special Scientific Interest (SSSI)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> The trees in Abbey Park were planted on Mount Hill in 1779 by <a href="/wiki/James_Bentham" title="James Bentham">James Bentham</a>, a minor <a href="/wiki/Canon_(priest)" title="Canon (priest)">canon</a> of Ely. Ely Castle once stood on Mount Hill, which was renamed Cherry Hill following the tree plantings by Bentham.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chettisham_Meadow" title="Chettisham Meadow">Chettisham Meadow</a> SSSI is a medieval <a href="/wiki/Ridge_and_furrow" title="Ridge and furrow">ridge and furrow</a> grassland about 0.6 miles (1&#160;km) north of the city centre.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> This site, one of the UK's best remaining examples of ridge and furrow agriculture, also contains protected species such as the <a href="/wiki/Anacamptis_morio" title="Anacamptis morio">Green-winged Orchid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Illustrated_London_News_Vol_XI_30_October_1847_p_277_Ely_Station.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Ely railway station 1847" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Illustrated_London_News_Vol_XI_30_October_1847_p_277_Ely_Station.jpg/220px-Illustrated_London_News_Vol_XI_30_October_1847_p_277_Ely_Station.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Illustrated_London_News_Vol_XI_30_October_1847_p_277_Ely_Station.jpg/330px-Illustrated_London_News_Vol_XI_30_October_1847_p_277_Ely_Station.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Illustrated_London_News_Vol_XI_30_October_1847_p_277_Ely_Station.jpg/440px-Illustrated_London_News_Vol_XI_30_October_1847_p_277_Ely_Station.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5280" data-file-height="2843" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Illustrated_London_News_Vol_XI_30_October_1847_p_277_Ely_Station.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wood_engraving" title="Wood engraving">Engraving</a><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;xvii&#93;</a></sup> from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Illustrated_London_News" title="The Illustrated London News">The Illustrated London News</a></i> of the station at Ely during the opening on 25 October 1847 of the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Lynn" title="King&#39;s Lynn">Lynn</a> and Ely Railway, now part of the <a href="/wiki/Fen_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Fen Line">Fen Line</a><sup id="cite_ref-ILN1847_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILN1847-202">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rail">Rail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Rail">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Railways_in_Ely" title="Railways in Ely">Railways in Ely</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ely_railway_station" title="Ely railway station">Ely railway station</a>, on the <a href="/wiki/Fen_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Fen Line">Fen Line</a>, is a major railway hub with the <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_railway_station" title="Cambridge railway station">Cambridge</a> to Ely section opening in 1845. Five major railway lines—excluding the former <a href="/wiki/Ely_and_St_Ives_Railway" title="Ely and St Ives Railway">Ely and St Ives Railway</a>—emanate from this hub: north to <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Lynn" title="King&#39;s Lynn">King's Lynn</a>, northwest to <a href="/wiki/Peterborough" title="Peterborough">Peterborough</a>, east to <a href="/wiki/Norwich" title="Norwich">Norwich</a>, southeast to <a href="/wiki/Ipswich" title="Ipswich">Ipswich</a> and south to Cambridge and London.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> At the opening of the <span class="frac nowrap">26<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span>-mile (42.6&#160;km) <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Lynn" title="King&#39;s Lynn">Lynn</a> and Ely railway "with great éclat"<sup id="cite_ref-ILN1847_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILN1847-202">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> on 25 October 1847, the Ely station building,<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;xviii&#93;</a></sup> completed in 1847,<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> was described by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Illustrated_London_News" title="The Illustrated London News">The Illustrated London News</a></i> as "an extensive pile<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;xix&#93;</a></sup> in pleasing mixed <a href="/wiki/Greek_Revival_architecture" title="Greek Revival architecture">Grecian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Italy" title="Architecture of Italy">Italian</a> style".<sup id="cite_ref-ILN1847_202-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILN1847-202">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> The former Ely and St&#160;Ives Railway, known locally as the Grunty Fen Express,<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> opened in 1865 but was never popular. In 1866, the <span class="frac nowrap">7<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span>-mile (12.1&#160;km) return journey from Ely to <a href="/wiki/Sutton-in-the-Isle" title="Sutton-in-the-Isle">Sutton-in-the-Isle</a> cost two <a href="/wiki/Shilling_(British_coin)" title="Shilling (British coin)">shillings</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> which equates to a cost of almost £9<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;xx&#93;</a></sup> in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-UK_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-UK-211">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> The line closed to passengers in February 1931 and completely closed in 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> As of December 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, train operating companies using Ely were: <a href="/wiki/Thameslink_and_Great_Northern" class="mw-redirect" title="Thameslink and Great Northern">Great Northern</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greater_Anglia_(train_operating_company)" title="Greater Anglia (train operating company)">Greater Anglia</a>, <a href="/wiki/CrossCountry" title="CrossCountry">CrossCountry</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands_Railway" title="East Midlands Railway">East Midlands Railway</a> with direct trains to Cambridge, London, most of <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/English_Midlands" class="mw-redirect" title="English Midlands">Midlands</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">North</a>. There are connecting services to many other parts of England and to Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Road">Road</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Road">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Roman_roads_in_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman roads in Britain">Roman road</a>, named Akeman Street,<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;xxi&#93;</a></sup> has been documented from <a href="/wiki/Ermine_Street" title="Ermine Street">Ermine Street</a> near <a href="/wiki/Wimpole" title="Wimpole">Wimpole</a> through Cambridge, <a href="/wiki/Stretham" title="Stretham">Stretham</a> and Ely to <a href="/wiki/Brancaster" title="Brancaster">Brancaster</a> through <a href="/wiki/Denver,_Norfolk" title="Denver, Norfolk">Denver</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> This is not the same road as the major Roman road named <a href="/wiki/Akeman_Street" title="Akeman Street">Akeman Street</a> which started from <i>Verulamium</i> (southwest of <a href="/wiki/St_Albans" title="St Albans">St Albans</a>) then via <a href="/wiki/Tring" title="Tring">Tring</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aylesbury" title="Aylesbury">Aylesbury</a> terminating near <a href="/wiki/Alcester" title="Alcester">Alcester</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> In Bishop John Fordham's survey of Ely in 1416–1417, an east to west <i>Akermanstrete</i> or <i>Agemanstrete</i> is mentioned, which now forms part of the east–west Egremont Street.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Akeman Street would have been oriented north-south passing through central Ely and therefore the east–west oriented Egremont Street cannot have a Roman origin.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> It is suggested that the Wimpole to Brancaster road name of "Akeman" was derived by <a href="/wiki/Antiquarian" title="Antiquarian">antiquarians</a>, without justification, from <i>Acemanes-ceastre</i>, an ancient name for <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boundary_Marker_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1714439.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Turnpike marker 1852 showing southwest boundary of Ely" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Boundary_Marker_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1714439.jpg/170px-Boundary_Marker_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1714439.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Boundary_Marker_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1714439.jpg/255px-Boundary_Marker_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1714439.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Boundary_Marker_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1714439.jpg/340px-Boundary_Marker_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1714439.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boundary_Marker_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1714439.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Turnpike marker 1852 showing southwest boundary of Ely</div></div></div>
<p>Medieval accountant, Clement of Thetford made, or had others make on his behalf, many journeys between 1291 and 1292, as evidenced by his <a href="/wiki/Sacristan" title="Sacristan">sacrist's</a> rolls—the earliest known roll of the Ely Monastery.<sup id="cite_ref-SacristRolls_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SacristRolls-221">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> For example, he travelled the 25 miles (40&#160;km)<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;xxii&#93;</a></sup> to Bury (<a href="/wiki/Bury_St_Edmunds" title="Bury St Edmunds">Bury St Edmunds</a>) fair to obtain rice, sugar, etc., the 16 miles (26&#160;km) to <a href="/wiki/Barnwell,_Cambridgeshire" title="Barnwell, Cambridgeshire">Barnwell</a> for wheels, axles, etc. for carts, then the 51 miles (82&#160;km) to St&#160;Botolph's (<a href="/wiki/Boston,_Lincolnshire" title="Boston, Lincolnshire">Boston</a>) for wine, the 14 miles (23&#160;km) to Reche (<a href="/wiki/Reach,_Cambridgeshire" title="Reach, Cambridgeshire">Reach</a>) for steel and iron and the 78 miles (126&#160;km) to London, principally for things needed in the vestry for the service of the Church, but also to pay taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-SacristRolls_221-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SacristRolls-221">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> Some or parts of these journeys will have been made by river.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The eighteenth-century historian <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Carter_(topographer)" title="Edmund Carter (topographer)">Edmund Carter</a>, in his 1753 <i>History of the County of Cambridge &amp;c.</i>, described a thrice-weekly coach journey "for the conveniency of sending and receiving letters and small parcels" from the Lamb Inn, Ely to the post-house, Cambridge.<sup id="cite_ref-Carter_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carter-224">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> In the 1760s, the Reverend James Bentham, an <a href="/wiki/Antiquarian" title="Antiquarian">antiquarian</a> and minor canon of Ely, encouraged the ecclesiastical authorities and townspeople of Ely to subscribe<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;xxiii&#93;</a></sup> to a <a href="/wiki/Turnpike_trust" class="mw-redirect" title="Turnpike trust">turnpike road</a> between Ely and Cambridge; improvements which started in 1769.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> The eighteenth century London to King's Lynn coach route, documented by the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Postmaster_General" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Postmaster General">Postmaster General</a>'s surveyor, <a href="/wiki/John_Cary" title="John Cary">John Cary</a>, passed through Ely with a stop at the Lamb Inn, a coaching inn in 1753<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> and extant as the Lamb Hotel.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Cary measured the distance of the London (<a href="/wiki/Shoreditch" title="Shoreditch">Shoreditch</a>) to Ely section as being 67 miles (108&#160;km).<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/A142_road#Triple-digit_roads" title="A142 road">A142</a> road from <a href="/wiki/Newmarket,_Suffolk" title="Newmarket, Suffolk">Newmarket</a> to <a href="/wiki/Chatteris" title="Chatteris">Chatteris</a> passes east–west, south of the town.
</p><p>Ely is on the north-south <a href="/wiki/A10_road_(England)" title="A10 road (England)">A10</a> road from London to King's Lynn; the southwestern bypass of the town was built in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> A proposal for an Ely southeast bypass of the A142 is included in the major schemes of the Cambridgeshire <a href="/wiki/Local_Transport_Plan" class="mw-redirect" title="Local Transport Plan">Local Transport Plan</a>. The proposed route would include 1.2 miles (1.9&#160;km) of new road between new roundabout junctions on Stuntney Causeway and Angel Drove. The bypass is intended to reduce congestion in Ely, and to avoid the low bridge on the Ely to King's Lynn railway line, which has the third highest vehicle strike rate in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> Proposals for the bypass went to public consultation in October 2011 and the County Council and District Council have announced that they may fund some of the costs of construction (estimated to be up to £28&#160;million)<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> with contributions from developers who wish to build a retail park near the proposed route.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The bypass, completed at £13m over budget, opened on 31 October 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="River">River</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: River">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/River_Cam" title="River Cam">River Cam</a> flows through the southeastern boundary of the city. <a href="/wiki/Cnut_the_Great" title="Cnut the Great">King Cnut</a> arrived at Ely by boat for the Purification of St&#160;Mary; "When they were approaching land [at Ely], the king rose up in the middle of his men and directed the boatmen to make for the little port<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;xxiv&#93;</a></sup> at full speed".<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> Archaeological excavations in the year 2000, between Broad Street and the present river, revealed artificially cut channels "at right-angles to the present river front" thus "evidently part of the medieval port of Ely".<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> In 1753, Carter reports that "for the conveniency of passengers, and heavy goods to and from Cambridge" a boat left Ely every Tuesday and Friday for Cambridge; the 20-mile (32&#160;km) journey took six hours.<sup id="cite_ref-Carter_224-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carter-224">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_sites">Religious sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Religious sites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ely_Cathedral">Ely Cathedral</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Ely Cathedral">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ely_Cathedral_by_John_Buckler.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Northeast aspect of Ely Cathedral" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Ely_Cathedral_by_John_Buckler.JPG/220px-Ely_Cathedral_by_John_Buckler.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Ely_Cathedral_by_John_Buckler.JPG/330px-Ely_Cathedral_by_John_Buckler.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Ely_Cathedral_by_John_Buckler.JPG/440px-Ely_Cathedral_by_John_Buckler.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1940" data-file-height="1361" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ely_Cathedral_by_John_Buckler.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Northeast aspect of Ely Cathedral. The Lady Chapel, built between 1335 and 1353, is to the right of the image. Early 19th-century proof-print by <a href="/wiki/John_Buckler" title="John Buckler">John Buckler</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pevsner_1977_340_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pevsner_1977_340-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ely_Cathedral" title="Ely Cathedral">Ely Cathedral</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Ely" title="Diocese of Ely">Diocese of Ely</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> Cathedral Church of the Holy and Undivided Trinity is known as the Ship of the Fens,<sup id="cite_ref-EDP24_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EDP24-240">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> a name inspired by the distant views of its towers, which dominate the low-lying wetlands known as "The Fens".<sup id="cite_ref-Pevsnerview_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pevsnerview-76">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The diocese of Ely was created in 1108 out of the <a href="/wiki/See_of_Lincoln" class="mw-redirect" title="See of Lincoln">see of Lincoln</a>, and a year later the bishopric of Ely was founded. Construction of the cathedral was begun by <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a> in 1083, with it finally opening in 1189 after 116 years.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> On 22&#160;February 1322 it suffered the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Crossing_(architecture)" title="Crossing (architecture)">crossing</a>, which was rebuilt as an octagon.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> The cathedral was completed in 1351. <a href="/wiki/John_Wesley" title="John Wesley">John Wesley</a> wrote of his 22&#160;November 1774 visit to Ely that "the cathedral, [is] one of the most beautiful I have seen. The western tower is exceedingly grand, and the nave of an amazing height".<sup id="cite_ref-Wesley_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wesley-109">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ely is the nearest cathedral city to Cambridge, which lies within the same diocese. The Diocese of Ely covers 1,507 square miles (3,903&#160;km<sup>2</sup>), 641,000 people (2011) and 335 churches. It includes the county of Cambridgeshire, except for much of <a href="/wiki/Peterborough" title="Peterborough">Peterborough</a>, and three parishes in the south which are in the diocese of <a href="/wiki/Chelmsford" title="Chelmsford">Chelmsford</a>. The Diocese of Ely also includes the western part of <a href="/wiki/Norfolk" title="Norfolk">Norfolk</a>, a few parishes in Peterborough and <a href="/wiki/Essex" title="Essex">Essex</a>, and one in <a href="/wiki/Bedfordshire" title="Bedfordshire">Bedfordshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_churches">Other churches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Other churches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The church of St&#160;Mary, dedicated by <a href="/wiki/Eustace_(Bishop_of_Ely)" class="mw-redirect" title="Eustace (Bishop of Ely)">Bishop Eustace</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Pevsner_1977_379_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pevsner_1977_379-175">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> is an early thirteenth-century building with a <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>&#160;1300 spire and tower with eight bells. The church was heavily restored starting in 1877.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/St_Etheldreda%27s_Church,_Ely" title="St Etheldreda&#39;s Church, Ely">The Roman Catholic Church of St. Etheldreda</a>, in Egremont Street, dates from 1891.<sup id="cite_ref-Churches_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Churches-246">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> The Methodist chapel, in Chapel Street, was built in 1818 and was restored in 1891.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> The Salem Baptist chapel was erected in 1840.<sup id="cite_ref-Churches_246-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Churches-246">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> The Church of <a href="/wiki/St_Peter-in-Ely" title="St Peter-in-Ely">St Peter-in-Ely</a> on Broad Street was built in 1890; the architect was <a href="/wiki/James_Piers_St_Aubyn" title="James Piers St Aubyn">James Piers St Aubyn</a><sup id="cite_ref-Pevsner_1977_380_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pevsner_1977_380-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> and it contains a fine <a href="/wiki/Ninian_Comper" title="Ninian Comper">Ninian Comper</a> <a href="/wiki/Rood_screen" title="Rood screen">rood screen</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Countess_Free_Church,_Ely" title="Countess Free Church, Ely">Countess Free Church</a> is part of the <a href="/wiki/Countess_of_Huntingdon%27s_Connexion" title="Countess of Huntingdon&#39;s Connexion">Countess of Huntingdon's Connexion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-countessely1_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-countessely1-249">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fen_skating" title="Fen skating">Fen skating</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ely_City_F.C." title="Ely City F.C.">Ely City F.C.</a> was established in 1885<sup id="cite_ref-goalrun_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goalrun-250">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> and joined the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Counties_Football_League" title="Eastern Counties Football League">Eastern Counties Football League</a> in 1960. In the 1997–1998 season, they reached the 3rd round of the <a href="/wiki/FA_Vase" title="FA Vase">FA Vase</a>. Since the 2007–08 season they have been members of the Eastern Counties Football League Premier Division.<sup id="cite_ref-goalrun_250-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goalrun-250">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1973, Ely won the international <i><a href="/wiki/Jeux_Sans_Fronti%C3%A8res" title="Jeux Sans Frontières">Jeux Sans Frontières</a></i> competition (known in Britain as <i><a href="/wiki/It%27s_a_Knockout" title="It&#39;s a Knockout">It's a Knockout</a>!</i>), becoming the last British town to win the title outright (<a href="/wiki/Dartmouth,_Devon" title="Dartmouth, Devon">Dartmouth</a> finished joint-first with <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> in 1981).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a> rowing club has a boathouse on the bank of the river, and trains there for the annual <a href="/wiki/The_Boat_Race" title="The Boat Race">Boat Race</a> against the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CUBC_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CUBC-251">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> In 1944, the Boat Race took place on a <span class="frac nowrap">1<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span>-mile (2.4&#160;km) course on the <a href="/wiki/River_Great_Ouse" title="River Great Ouse">River Great Ouse</a> near Ely, the only time it has not been held on the <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">River Thames</a>. On that occasion the race was won by Oxford.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC2004_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2004-252">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> The Isle of Ely Rowing Club was formed to commemorate the 60th anniversary of this event.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A short lived <a href="/wiki/Greyhound_racing_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Greyhound racing in the United Kingdom">greyhound racing</a> track was opened on Saturday 13 May 1933 at the Downham Road Stadium. The racing was independent (not affiliated to the sports governing body the <a href="/wiki/National_Greyhound_Racing_Club" title="National Greyhound Racing Club">National Greyhound Racing Club</a>) and was known as a flapping track, which was the nickname given to independent tracks.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/King%27s_Ely" title="King&#39;s Ely">King's Ely</a> claims to have beginnings in the re-foundation of St&#160;Etheldreda's monastery in 970 by the <a href="/wiki/Benedictine" class="mw-redirect" title="Benedictine">Benedictine</a> order. The wealthy sent their sons to such places to learn how to read and translate Latin texts. <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a> may<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;xxv&#93;</a></sup> have been educated at Ely between <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>&#160;1005 and 1010. "The teaching of grammar continued in the cloisters [of Ely] and this tradition was the forerunner of the Cathedral Grammar School, today known as The King's School Ely".<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Needham's Charity School was founded in 1740 in Back Hill by Mrs Catherine Needham 'for the education, clothing and apprenticement of poor children'.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-259">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> There were originally 24 free scholars aged 9 to 14 years of age.' The Needham's School relocated to a new building in Downham Road adjacent to Ely High School in 1969. This building is now part of Ely College.
</p><p>The National School for boys was located in Silver Street. There was a National School for Girls in Market Street. Both National Schools received bursaries from the Parson's Charity.The Broad Street School was erected in 1858.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_259-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-259">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> In later years the Silver Street and Broad Street schools operated as St Mary's Junior School. The <a href="/wiki/Ely_High_School_for_Girls" title="Ely High School for Girls">Ely High School for Girls</a> opened in 1905 in St. Mary's Street, moving to the Downham Road site in 1957. In 1972 Ely High School closed when state secondary education in the area changed to the comprehensive model, the site becoming the City of Ely VIth Form College, part of Ely Community College which is currently Ely College.
</p><p>In 1940 the Bishop's Palace was acquired by the Red Cross as a hospital and after the second world war it became a school for disabled children and young people.
</p><p>Education in Ely, as of 2017, includes: <a href="/wiki/The_King%27s_School,_Ely" class="mw-redirect" title="The King&#39;s School, Ely">The King's School</a>; <a href="/wiki/Ely_College" title="Ely College">Ely College</a>; The Lantern Community Primary School; St Mary's CofE Junior School; Isle of Ely Primary School; St&#160;John's Community Primary School and Highfield Special Academy.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_services">Public services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Public services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Anglian Water supplies the city's <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">water</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sewage_collection_and_disposal" class="mw-redirect" title="Sewage collection and disposal">sewage</a> services from their Ely Public Water Supply.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;xxvi&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Water_quality" title="Water quality">water quality</a> was reported as excellent in 2011. In the same report, the <a href="/wiki/Hard_water" title="Hard water">hardness</a> was reported as 292&#160;mg/l. The nearest reservoir, <a href="/wiki/Grafham_Water" title="Grafham Water">Grafham Water</a>, is 21 miles (34&#160;km) due west.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Distribution_Network_Operator" class="mw-redirect" title="Distribution Network Operator">Distribution Network Operator</a> for <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity</a> is <a href="/wiki/EDF_Energy" title="EDF Energy">EDF Energy</a>. The largest straw-burning power station in the world is at nearby Sutton. This <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> resource power station rated at 36.85&#160;<a href="/wiki/Megawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Megawatt">MW</a> from burning <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a>, nearly 25&#160;percent of the total renewable energy reported for Cambridgeshire in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-renewable_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-renewable-263">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> The world's largest poultry litter power plant, 38.5&#160;MW, is at <a href="/wiki/Thetford" title="Thetford">Thetford</a>, Norfolk.<sup id="cite_ref-lords_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lords-264">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>East Cambridgeshire District Council is part of the Recycling in Cambridgeshire and Peterborough (RECAP) Partnership, which was granted <a href="/wiki/Beacon_Council_(award)" title="Beacon Council (award)">Beacon status</a> for waste and recycling in 2006–07.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> (RAF) Hospital on Lynn Road, north of Ely, was built during the Second World War, and until 1993 served nearby permanent RAF stations including at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Marham" title="RAF Marham">Marham</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Feltwell" title="RAF Feltwell">Feltwell</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Lakenheath" title="RAF Lakenheath">Lakenheath</a>, and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Mildenhall" title="RAF Mildenhall">Mildenhall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Royal_Highness" title="Royal Highness">HRH</a> <a href="/wiki/Diana,_Princess_of_Wales" title="Diana, Princess of Wales">Diana, Princess of Wales</a> renamed the hospital in 1987, and since 1993 the Princess of Wales Hospital sees 40,000 outpatient visitors per year as a community hospital; part of the lifespan healthcare <a href="/wiki/NHS_care_trust" title="NHS care trust">NHS trust</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Services</a> (NHS) for the city are administered (2011) by <a href="/wiki/NHS_East_of_England" title="NHS East of England">NHS East of England</a>. Acute cases are handled by four hospitals, including <a href="/wiki/Addenbrooke%27s_Hospital" title="Addenbrooke&#39;s Hospital">Addenbrooke's Hospital</a>, 20 miles (32&#160;km) south and <a href="/wiki/Papworth_Hospital" class="mw-redirect" title="Papworth Hospital">Papworth Hospital</a> 28 miles (45&#160;km) southwest of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_people">Notable people</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Notable people">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DawnRiverGreatOusEly.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Dawn by the river Great Ouse, Ely" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/DawnRiverGreatOusEly.JPG/220px-DawnRiverGreatOusEly.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/DawnRiverGreatOusEly.JPG/330px-DawnRiverGreatOusEly.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/DawnRiverGreatOusEly.JPG/440px-DawnRiverGreatOusEly.JPG 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DawnRiverGreatOusEly.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dawn by the <a href="/wiki/River_Great_Ouse" title="River Great Ouse">River Great Ouse</a>, Ely</div></div></div>
<p>The former RAF hospital based in Ely meant that many children of serving RAF parents were born in the city, including <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a> player and <a href="/wiki/Rugby_World_Cup_2003" class="mw-redirect" title="Rugby World Cup 2003">Rugby World Cup 2003</a> winning manager with <a href="/wiki/England_national_rugby_union_team" title="England national rugby union team">England national rugby team</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clive_Woodward" title="Clive Woodward">Clive Woodward</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australian</a> émigrée actor <a href="/wiki/Guy_Pearce" title="Guy Pearce">Guy Pearce</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> and actors <a href="/wiki/Simon_MacCorkindale" title="Simon MacCorkindale">Simon MacCorkindale</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Westhead" title="David Westhead">David Westhead</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Autogyro" title="Autogyro">Autogyro</a> world record-holder <a href="/wiki/Ken_Wallis" title="Ken Wallis">Ken Wallis</a> was also born in Ely. Other notable people from Ely include <a href="/wiki/The_Sisters_of_Mercy" title="The Sisters of Mercy">The Sisters of Mercy</a> singer <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Eldritch" title="Andrew Eldritch">Andrew Eldritch</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> and journalist <a href="/wiki/Chris_Hunt" title="Chris Hunt">Chris Hunt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EoLChunt_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EoLChunt-273">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> Crime writer <a href="/wiki/Jim_Kelly_(author)" title="Jim Kelly (author)">Jim Kelly</a><sup id="cite_ref-Penguin_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penguin-274">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> and award-winning poet <a href="/wiki/Wendy_Cope" title="Wendy Cope">Wendy Cope</a><sup id="cite_ref-EWNCope_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EWNCope-275">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> currently live in the city.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ely,_Cambridgeshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Children's book <a href="/wiki/Tom%27s_Midnight_Garden" title="Tom&#39;s Midnight Garden">Tom's Midnight Garden</a> by <a href="/wiki/Philippa_Pearce" title="Philippa Pearce">Philippa Pearce</a> is partly set in Ely and includes a scene in Ely Cathedral and scenes inspired by the author's father's own childhood experiences of skating along the frozen river from Cambridge to Ely in the frost of 1894–95.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PPMCDWood_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PPMCDWood-277">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PPMPPullman_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PPMPPullman-278">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> The first series of <a href="/wiki/Jim_Kelly_(author)" title="Jim Kelly (author)">Jim Kelly's</a> crime novels, featuring journalist Philip Dryden, is largely set in the author's home town of Ely and in the Fens.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Graham_Swift" title="Graham Swift">Graham Swift</a>'s 1983 novel <a href="/wiki/Waterland_(novel)" title="Waterland (novel)">Waterland</a> takes place, and recounts several historical events, in and around the town of Ely.
</p><p>The album cover for <a href="/wiki/Pink_Floyd" title="Pink Floyd">Pink Floyd</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Division_Bell" title="The Division Bell">The Division Bell</a></i> was created by <a href="/wiki/Storm_Thorgerson" title="Storm Thorgerson">Storm Thorgerson</a> with Ely as the background between two massive sculptures that he had erected outside the city.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<li id="cite_note-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-130">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The word coprolite is from the Greek <i>kopros</i> which means dung and <i>lithos</i> which means stone. The word was first coined in 1829 by Rev. <a href="/wiki/William_Buckland" title="William Buckland">William Buckland</a> and is a misnomer as the nodules are fossilised bone<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup></span>

<ul><li>Historical documents relating to Ely, including Church of England parish registers, court records, maps and photographs, are held by <a href="/wiki/Cambridgeshire_Archives_and_Local_Studies" title="Cambridgeshire Archives and Local Studies">Cambridgeshire Archives and Local Studies</a> at the County Record Office in Cambridge.</li>

<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ely.org.uk">Ely Online</a> – the longest-established website about Ely which has been online since 1996.</li>
